information,orpropositions,orassertions,orstatements,orhypotheses. 


Foralongtimeitwasthought(andisstillthoughtbymanyeminent 
mathematiciansandphysicists)53 
thatwemaystartfromasubjectively 
interpretedsystemofprobabilisticpremissesandthenderivefromthese 
subjectivistpremissesobjectivestatisticalconclusions.Thisis,however,a 
gravelogicalblunder. 


Theblunder[orrather,theprehistoryoftheblunder,andnot,asB.L.van 
derWaerdentellsme54 
,theblunderitself]maybetracedbacktosomeofthe 
greatfoundersofprobabilitytheory,toJacobBernoulliandespeciallySiméon 
DenisPoisson,whothoughtthattheyhaddiscovered,intheirderivationsof 
thevariousformsofthelawofgreatnumbers,akindoflogico-mathematical 
bridgeleadingfromnon-statisticalassumptionstostatisticalconclusions;that 
is,toconclusionsconcerningthefrequencyofcertainevents. 


ThelogicalmistakewascarefullyanalysedbyRichardvonMises55and 
alsobymyself.56Misesshowedthatatsomestageorotherinthederivation 
thenon-statisticalmeaningofthesymbolsisdroppedandtacitlyreplacedby 
astatisticalone.Thisisusuallydonebyinterpretingaprobabilityapproaching 
1as‘almostcertain’inthesenseof‘almostalwaystohappen’,insteadof 
‘almostcertain’inthesenseof‘verystronglybelievedin’orperhaps‘almost 
known’.Sometimesthemistakeconsistsinreplacing‘almostcertainlyknown’ 
by‘knownalmostcertainlytooccur’.Howeverthismaybe,themistakeis 
veryclear:frompremissesaboutdegreesofbeliefwecannevergeta 
conclusionaboutthefrequencyofevents. 


Itisstrangethatthisideathatwecanderivestatisticalconclusionsfrom 
premissesexpressinguncertaintyisstillsostrongamongquantumtheorists; 
forJohnvonNeumann,oneofthemostinfluentialamongthem,acceptedin 
hisfamousbook,MathematicalFoundationsofQuantumMechanics,the 
theoryofprobabilityofvonMises.57YetvonNeumann’spraiseofthistheory

Mises’sargumentsagainsttheexistenceofa‘bridge’fromnon-statistical 



premissestostatisticalconclusions. 


IdonotwishtoimplythatIacceptvonMises’stheoryasawhole;butI 
believethathiscriticismofthealleged‘bridge’fromnon-statisticalpremisses 
tostatisticalconclusionsisunanswerable;andIdonotevenknowofany

nameof‘Bayesianprobability’,iswidelyanduncriticallyaccepted. 


Inowproceedtotheobjectiveinterpretationsoftheprobabilitycalculus.I 
shallheredistinguishbetweenthreesuchinterpretations: 


(a)Theclassicalinterpretation(deMoivre,Laplace)whichtakesp(a,b)to 
betheproportionofequallypossiblecasescompatiblewiththeeventb 
whicharealsofavourabletotheeventa.Forexample,letabetheevent 
‘atthenextthrowofthisdie5willturnup’andtakebtobethe 
assumption‘6willnotturnup’(or‘onlythrowsotherthan6willbe 
consideredasthrows’);thenp(a,b)=1/5. 


(b) 
Thefrequencyinterpretationorstatisticalinterpretation(JohnVenn, 
GeorgeHelm,vonMises)whichtakesp(a,b)astherelativefrequency 
oftheeventsaamongtheeventsb.Thisinterpretation,whichI 
developedbytryingtoremovesomeofitsdifficulties,58isonewhichI 
upheldforabouttwentyyears(fromapproximately1930to1950), 
thoughIalwaysstressedtheexistenceofotherinterpretations.59 
(c)ThepropensityinterpretationwhichIdevelopedfromacriticismofmy 
ownformofthefrequencyinterpretationandwhichmayatthesame

Ishallhavetosayafewthingsabouteachofthethreeobjective

Infavourof(a),theclassicalinterpretation,itmaybesaidthatitisused, 
almostasamatterofcourseandobviouslywithgoodreason,insituations

Ontheotherhand,theclassicalinterpretationhasbeenseverelycriticized 
onseveralcounts,ofwhichIwillmentiononlytwo.(1)Asitstandsitis

Asto(b),thefrequencyinterpretation,IfeelconfidentthatIhave

ThusIcometo(c),tothepropensityinterpretationofprobability. 


Nothingisfurtherfrommymindthananattempttosolvethepseudoproblemofgivingadefinitionofthemeaningofprobability.
Itisobviousthat 
theword‘probability’canbeusedperfectlyproperlyandlegitimatelyin

Whetherthepropensityinterpretationisapplicabletoallcasesofbetting

Ishallexplainthepropensityinterpretationasadevelopmentofthe 
classicalinterpretation.Thelatter,itwillberemembered,explainsp(a,b)— 
thatis,theprobabilityofagivenb—astheproportionofthoseequallypossible 
casessatisfyingbwhicharefavourabletotheeventa. 


Ipropose,asafirststep,toomittheword‘equally’andtointroduce 
‘weights’,andthustospeak,insteadof‘numbersofcases’,ofthe‘sumofthe 
weightsofthecases’.AndIpropose,asasecondstep,tointerpretthese 
‘weights’ofthepossibilities(orofthepossiblecases)asmeasuresofthe 
propensity,ortendency,ofapossibilitytorealizeitselfuponrepetition. 


ThusIarriveatthefollowing: 


Theformulaep(a,b),or‘theprobability(orpropensity)ofagivenb’,may 
beinterpretedas‘the‘sumoftheweightsofthepossiblecasesthatsatisfythe 
conditionbwhicharealsofavourabletoa,dividedbythesumoftheweights 
ofthepossiblecasesthatsatisfyb’. 


Themainideaofthisinterpretationcanalsobeputasfollows:Iproposeto

inwhichquantummechanicsdiffersfundamentallyfromclassicalphysics— 
thatis,intheinterferenceofthepropensitywaves—showsthatthepropensity 
wavescaninteractandarethereforereal.Thisisapowerfulargumentforthe 
existenceofpropensityfields;andifitisacceptedweareabletoexplainthe 
mostcharacteristicpeculiaritiesofquantumtheory. 


Ontheotherhand,itseemstomeclearfromtheCompton-Simon 
photographsthatphotonscanbekickedandcankickback,andaretherefore 



(inspiteofLande’sscepticalviewsastotheirexistence)‘real’inpreciselythe 
sensewhichLandehimselfhasgiventotheterm.[Admittedly,theyareless 
realthan,say,electrons,becausephotonnumbersarenotconservedwhile 
electronnumbersareconservedatleastinwhatmaybecalled‘ordinary’ 
processes(not,ofcourse,inpairannihilation).84Butafterall,tablesandchairs 
andhumanbodiesarenotpreservedeither(especiallynottheirnumbers);nor 
areatoms.] 


Asalways,nothingdependsonwords,buttalkingof‘dualismofparticleand 
wave’hascreatedmuchconfusion,asLanderightlyemphasizes:somuchso 
thatIwishtosupporthissuggestiontoabandontheterm‘dualism’.Ipropose 
thatwespeakinstead(asdidEinstein)oftheparticleandits‘associated’ 
propensityfields(thepluralindicatesthatthefieldsdependnotonlyonthe 
particlebutalsoonotherconditions),thusavoidingthesuggestionofa 
symmetricalrelation. 


Withoutestablishingsomesuchterminologyasthis(‘association’inplace 
of 
‘dualism’)

is

theassociationthatexistsbetweenparticlesandfieldsofpropensities(‘forces’, 
decaypropensities,propensitiesforpairproduction,andothers). 


Incidentally,amongthemisleadingfashionabletermsofthetheoryisthe 
term‘observable’.85Itsuggestssomethingthatdoesnotexist:all‘observables’ 
arecalculatedandinferredontheoreticalgrounds,ratherthanobservedor 
directlymeasured.Thuswhatis‘observable’alwaysdependsuponthetheory 
weuse.However,hereagainoneshouldnotquarrelaboutwords;nomore 
abouttheword‘observable’thanabouttheword‘real’.Definitions,asusual, 
leadnowhere;butmostofusknowwhatwemeanwhenwesaythatthereare 
suchthings(observablethings)aselephants,orelectrons,ormagneticfieldsor 
(moredifficulttoobserve)propensities,suchasthepropensitytoattract,orto 
understand,ortocriticize;orthepropensityofanexperimenttoyieldsome 
specifiedresult. 


Tosumup.Theallegeddualismofparticleandwaveandthesubjective

4TheCollapseoftheWavePacketwithoutObservation. 


*(Added1980)Theargumentofthesis9is,essentially,thatofmyL.Sc.D.of 
1934.Itdealsespeciallywiththeso-called‘reductionofthewavepacket’or, 
whatisthesame,the‘collapseofthestatevector’,anditcanbemuch 
strengthened. 


Asitstands,itreferstotheimpactofanynewinformationona 
probability:itshowsthataprobabilityp(a,b)maybereplaced,onthenew 
informationc,byp(a,bc).Anditshowsthat,ifaisthefinalpositionofaball 
onthepinboard,theinformationcthattheballhasactuallyhitacertainpin 
mayofcourseconsiderablyaltertheprobabilityofa;itmay,forexample, 
reduceittozero. 


Inthisargument,theterm‘information’playsaconsiderablepart;andfor 
thisreason,onemaysaythatithasacertainsubjectivistflavour.Itmayseem 
that‘theobserver’hasnotbeencompletelyexorcized.Themainpurposeof 
thepresentsectionistoshowthat,withthehelpofthepropensity 
interpretationofprobability(whichIdidnotdevelopuntilshortlybefore 
1953)90 
,theexorcismistotal. 


However,beforeproceedingtoshowthis,Iwishtosaysomethingin 
defenceoftheobjectivityoftheargumentinthesis9.Theinformationwhich 
playsapartinthatsectionandwhichleadstothereductionofthe‘wave 
packet’isinformationaboutobjectivefacts,factswhicharequiteindependent 
ofany‘observer’:thefactwhetherornot,forexample,aballhashitacertain 
pinofapinboard.Thustheobjectionthat,byspeakingofinformation,Ihave 
illicitlybroughtintheobserver,canhardlybeupheld.Nevertheless,wecan 
getridofanyreference,directorveiled,toinformation,ortoknowledge,or 
toanythingthathasanysubjectivistflavour. 


Taketheexampleofthepinboardagain.The‘regularpinboard’whichI 
amusinghereconsistsinaseriesoflinesofpins;andeachpinwithineach 
linehasaconstantdistancefromeveryotherpinwithinthatline.Theplacing 
ofthepinsineachsuccessivelineisstaggered,sothatitspinsareplaced 
midwaywithrespecttothoseontheprecedingline.Eachballisofsuchasize 
thatitcanjustpassthroughtheconstantdistancebetweenthepins. 



Theregularpinboardisofconsiderableimportancebecauseitcanserve 
asanextremelysimplemodelofaprobabilisticworld:aworldinwhichevery 
occurrence—thatis,aballhittingapin—givesrisetoaprobabilisticchoiceof 
occurrenceswithprobability½:awavepacketorastatevectorrepresenting 
twopossibleandequiprobablestates,eachofwhichhasagainthesame 
character,andsoonaslongaswelike,uptoa‘final’line,inwhichwechoose 
acertainpin.Hittingthispinisouroccurrencea;p(a,x)istheprobabilityof 
hittingthischosenpinawhenthesituationisx. 


Nowthepropensityinterpretationregardspropensitiesasobjective 
physical 
properties 
of 
the 
physical 
situation 
under 
consideration 
and, 
ultimately,ofthewholephysicalworld.Accordingly,theobjectivepropensity 
oftheoccurrencea—describedbyp(a,x),awavepacketorastatevector— 
changeswheneveraballhas‘decided’tomoveleftofsomepin.Itchanges 
becausexchangeswitheveryoccurrenceobjectively. 


Whetherornotweare‘informed’oftheoccurrence(ofthevalueofx)is 
irrelevant:itistheobjectivepropensityofawhichmaybecomezero,withor 
withoutourknowledge. 


Sohereitisthephysicalworlditself—thepropensity—whichchanges,and 
whosechangeisdescribedbythe‘collapseofthestatevector’.Andthisisjust 
acharacteristicthatdistinguishesaprobabilisticworldfromadeterministic 



world—aworldinwhichtherearenoalternatives;ortoputitdifferently,in 
whichallobjectiveprobabilitiesorpropensitiesareeither1or0. 


Ibelievethatthistrivialmodelworld,thepinboard,isinmanyrespects 
verysimilartotheworldwhichquantummechanicsdepicts.Thereis,inthe 
main,onlyonedifference:inthemodeluniverse,wehavenophaseandno 
coherenceofphase.Thuswehavenointerferenceofprobabilities:our 
probabilitiescanadd,butnottheiramplitudes—aswasstressedinmyold 
theses8and9above. 


Wecan,ofcourse,trytosimulatethetwo-slitexperimentbyhavingballs 
comedownthepinboardthrough,say,thetwoslitsAandB.Butthiswill 
simplyleadtotheadditivesuperpositionoftwoGaussiandistributionsrather 
thantothesuperpositionsoftheamplitudesoftheprobabilitywaves.The 
reasonisobvious:wecannotinourmodelsimulateanythinglikephaseand 
coherence. 


Onethingwhichourmodelshowsveryclearlyisthetimesymmetry,that 
istosay,thefactthatthedirectionoftimemaybereversedinourmodel 
world.Ifweconsideranyparticularoccurrence—anyparticularhittingof 
somepinbysomeball—thenwecannotonlyaskforthedistributionofthe 
probabilitiesfortheball’sprogress,butalsoforthedistributionofthe 
probabilitiesfortheball’spastcourse.Weseeatoncethatthesetwoproblems 
areexactlysymmetrical:justasthereisaprobabilityof½foraballBthathas 
hitapinctohiteitherofthetwonearestpinsinthenextlineofpins,sothere 
isaprobabilityof½fortheballBtohavehit,beforehittingc,eitherofthe 
twonearestpinsinthepreviouslineofpins.Thusworkingbackwardsinthis 
wayweobtaindistributionswhichareexactlysymmetricaltothe(Gaussian) 
distributionswhichweobtaininworkingforward. 


This,Ithink,beautifullyillustratesthefamoustime-reversibilityofthe 
Schrödingerequation. 



5.Everett’s‘Many-Worlds’Interpretation. 


*(Added1981)Theseresultsmaybeappliedtoadiscussionofthefamous 
‘Many-mechanics,dueessentiallytoHughEverett,III,andinterestingly 
discussedbyJohnArchibaldWheeler,NeillGraham,andBryceS.DeWitt.91 


Inmyopinion,Everett’scontributionsareexcellent,althoughIdonot 
thinktheyaccomplishwhattheyaresupposedtodo—especiallyinthebook 
editedbyDeWittandGraham. 


Everett’sthreemaincontributionsappeartometobeasfollows: 


(1)Everett’sisacompletelyobjectivediscussionofquantummechanics. 


(2)InEverett’sapproach(asopposedtotheCopenhageninterpretation)there 
isnoneed,andnooccasion,todistinguishbetween‘classical’physical 
systems,likethemeasuringapparatus,andquantummechanicalsystems, 
likeelementaryparticlesand(smallish)systemsofelementaryparticles 
(say,moleculesorgroupsofmolecules).Instead,allphysicalsystemsare 
regardedasquantummechanicalsystems,especiallytheapparatususedin 
measurements;and,indeed,theuniverse. 


(3) 
Everettshowsthatthecollapseofthestatevector—somethingthathad, 
beforehim,beenregardedasoutsideSchrödinger’stheory—canbeshown 
toarisewithinwhathecalls‘thetheoryoftheUniveral[Schrödinger] 
WaveFunction’. 
ThethirdofthesepointsarisesfrommyowninterpretationofEverett’s 
theory.(Itiscontradicted,forexample,byDeWitt,whomaintainsthatthe

of

However,perhapsDeWittwishesonlytosaythatitdoesnot 
followunlessweadoptsomethinglikeEverett’sargument.)Onthefirsttwoof 
mythreepointsIshouldexpecteverybodytoagreethattheyareimportant 
aspectsofEverett’streatment. 


SeveralotheraspectsofEverett’streatment,oroftheinterpretationofhis 
treatmentbyDeWitt,seemtometobeduetomisunderstandings.Imay 
mentionafewofthese. 



Thereis,firstofall,theso-called‘many-worldsinterpretationofquantum 
mechanics’,orthesomewhatsensationalthesisthattheworldsplitsatany 
quantum-mechanicalinteractionintomanyworlds.Ishalltrybelowtoshow 
thatthisisuntenable. 


Thereis,secondly,theclaimthatEverett’sinterpretationisadirect 
consequence 
of 
the 
mathematical 
formalism, 
without 
any 
additional 
assumption(excepta‘postulateofcomplexity’asDeWittcallsit:‘Theworld 
is 
sufficiently

to 
be 
decomposable 
into 
systems 
and 
apparatuses’93).ItispartofthisclaimthatEverett’sinterpretationisfree 
of’metaphysical’assumptions.Thisclaim,too,willbeshowntobenottenable. 


However, 
my 
criticism 
should 
not 
be 
taken 
to 
belittle 
Everett’s 
achievementorhisoriginality. 


Everett’sargumentissimpleandingenious.Heconsidersthequantum 
mechanics 
of 
more 
or 
less 
complex 
systems 
which 
are 
regarded 
as 
(sufficiently)isolatedfromtherestofourworld.Whatholdsforthesesystems 
maythenbeextendedtothewholeuniverse. 


ThephysicalsystemsXwhicharespeciallyinvestigatedbyEverettare 
thosewhichcontainasubsystemAthatconsistsofanapparatus(acomputer) 
thatcanmeasuresomeoftheso-called‘observables’ofasubsystemBofX, 
and 
record 
the 
measurement 
in 
a 
physical 
subsystem 
of 
A, 
called, 
metaphorically,the‘memory’ofA. 


ThesubsystemBwhoseevolutionisrecordedbyAmaybe(forsimplicity) 
takentobeourregularpinboard;andAmaybetakentomeasureandto 
recordtheprobabilityofaballtoproceedononeofitspossiblepathsdown 
theboard.Theseprobabilitieswillberepresentedbya’wavepacket’or’state 
vector’;anda’measurement’(ofthepositionoftheball)mayormaynotbe 
the 
occurrence94 
of 
the 
ball 
hitting 
acertain 
pin. 
If 
it 
is, 
then 
the 
‘measurement’willleadtothe‘collapse’ofthepreviousstatevector,inthe 
sensesdescribedaboveinthesis9andinsection4. 


InsofarasthephysicalsystemArecordsthecollapseofthestatevector, 
itshowsthephysicalobjectivityofthiscollapse:thecollapseisshownby 
Everetttobepartoftheobjectivequantum-mechanicaltheory. 


Sofarsogood. 



ButEveretthasputaninterpretationonhisresults95 
whichIregardas 
definitelymetaphysical.Themotivationofthisinterpretationisverysimple. 
Thequantummechanicaltheory,sayintheformofSchrödinger’sequation, 
doesnotpredict(likeeveryprobabilistictheory)whichofthepossiblepaths 
opentoitthesystemwillchoose.Hesays—perhapsbelievingthatonlyinthis 
way 
will 
the 
objectivity 
of 
the 
theory 
be 
preserved—that 
‘with 
each 
succeedingobservation’thestateofthesystem‘branchesintoanumberof 
differentstates.Eachbranchrepresents’oneofthepossible‘different 
outcome[s]ofthemeasurementandthecorrespondingeigenstateforthe 
object-system[B’s]state.Allbranchesexistsimultaneously…afteranygiven 
sequenceofobservations.’ 


ThisviewofEverett’scanbeillustratedbyourpinboardmodel,enriched 
byanobservingandrecordingcomputerA.Itamountstotheassertionthat 
(asthestatevectordeterminingthenexttwopossiblestatesafterany 
‘measured’stateobjectivelydeterminestwostates)therewillbeasplitofour 
systemX(pinboardB+computerA,orA+B)intotwosystems:X′=A′+B′ 
andX″=A″+B″,eachofthemrealizingoneofthepredictedobjective 
possibilities.Ofcourse,inX′,A′willrecordastatevectorthatrepresentsthe 
state(futurepossibilities)ofB′whileinX″,A″willrecordthestatevectorthat 
representsthestate(futurepossibilities)ofB″. 


Inotherwords,ateveryoccurrence(aballhittingapin)oursystemX(i.e., 
board+computer)duplicates.(InamorecomplicatedsystemBwithmore 
thantwopossibilitiesopenedupbyaninteraction,thesystemXwillsplitinto 
nsystems,wherenisthenumberofpossibilitiesopenedupbytheoccurrence 
inB.) 


Eachofthemanyworlds(systems)followingasplit(anoccurrence) 
representsoneofthepossibleworlds—oneoftheworldswhichremain 
possibleaftertheoccurrence(themeasurement,say)thatledtothesplit. 


Clearly,sinceeachoftheseworldsisidenticalwithoneoftheworldsthat 
actuallycouldhavearisenaccordingtocommonsense,therewillbeno 
interactionbetweenthem:noinhabitantofoneofthemwillnoticeanything 
abouttheotherworlds,ornoticeanythingthatshouldnothappenina 
commonsenseworld. 



Therefore,Everett’sinterpretationisnotmadeimpossiblebythefactthat 
wedonotexperiencethecontinualsplittingofourworld(whichmeansthat 
weourselvessplitcontinually).Itis,ashepointsout,nomorecontradictedby 
ourfailuretoexperiencethesplittingthanthetheorythattheearthrotatesis 
contradictedbyourfailuretoexperienceitsmovement. 


Nevertheless,IthinkthatEverett’smany-worldsinterpretationcannot 
possiblybeupheld;notbecauseofitsconflictwithimmediateexperience,or 
withcommonsense,butbecauseofconflictswiththephysicaltheorywhich 
hethinkshefollowsfaithfully. 


Therearemanysuchconflicts.Somecanperhapsberesolvedby 
somethingverymuchlikespecialpleading.Butsomeare,inmyopinion, 
absolutelyfataltohistheory. 


Ofthoseconflictswhichcanberesolvedbyspecialpleadingarethose 
whichareconnectedwiththeconservationlaws—theconservationofenergy 
(energy-mass),ofmomentum,ofangularmomentum,etc.Theselawswould 
be,quiteobviously,drasticallyviolatedbyeverysplit. 


Thewayoutwouldbetosaythattheselawsholdwithineveryoneofthe 
branchingsystems,butnotofthesystemsofbranchingsystems. 


Thissurelycanbesaid,butthereisastrongobjectiontothiswayout:the 
onlyreasonforintroducingthesplits,andthemanyworlds,isthatapartof 
thephysicaltheory—theonewhichreferstothepossibilitieswhichasystem 
canadopt—canbetakenliterallyasadescriptionoftheactualtotalphysical 
realityratherthanofalternativepossibilitiesbetweenwhichthesystemcan 
choose(asinthecaseofthepinboard).Butthen,thewayoutmentionedsays 
thattheconservationlawsmustnotbetakenliterally—noteventhoseofthem 
whichareimpliedbythesameSchrödingerequationwhichisthebasisof 
Everett’stheory. 


Idonotthinkthatthiswayoutisatallacceptable. 


(Incidentally,weseeveryclearly,evenatthisstage,thatthemanyworlds 
interpretation 
does 
not, 
as 
claimed, 
follow 
directly 
from 
the 
physical 
formalism.) 


Butthereisanevenstrongercriticism. 


TheSchrödingerequationissymmetricalwithregardtoareversalofthe 



directionoftime—justasourregularpinboard,ashasbeenexplainedatthe 
endofsection4. 


ButthismeansthatifweacceptEverett’stheoryofsplits,thatistosay, 
therealityofeverypossibilitywithrespecttothefuture,wehavealsoto 
accepttheopposite—theconstantlyoccurringfusionofaninfinity96ofreal 
worldsatthetimeofeveryoccurrence.ThisisinevitableonEverett’stheory, 
foritmustpreserveoneofthemostimportantsymmetriesoftheformal 
theoryitissupposedtointerpret. 


Letusdenotethemomentoftimeatwhichwemakeastatepreparationor 
anobservationt1.TheordinaryuseofSchrödinger’sequationistoaskwhat 
willhappenatt2(wheret1<t2).AccordingtoEverett’sinterpretation,ifthere 
aretwopossibilities,eachletussaytobepredictedwiththeprobability½, 
thentheworldwillsplitintotwo,andeachprobabilitywillberealizedinone 
oftheworlds. 


ButwecanalsouseSchrödinger’sequationtoaskoutofwhatstates,att0, 
thestateatt1,maypossiblyhavearisen.Againtheanswermaybe‘outoftwo 
states,withtheprobabilityof½outofeach’.IthinkitisclearthatEverett’s 
interpretationwoulddemand,inthiscase,forreasonsofsymmetry,thatthe 
stateatt1arosefromthefusionofthestatesatt0.Sincetheformalismis 
symmetrical,theinterpretationhastobesymmetricalalso. 


Everettandhiscommentatorsdonotsayawordaboutthis;anditis, 
indeed,mostdifficulttosayanythingtodefendthisviewoftheworld— 
especiallyonceithasbeenshownthatthemany-worldsinterpretationisnota 
consequence(andthusnotanintegralpart)ofthequantummechanical 
formalism,but 
amoreorless 
(but 
admittedly 
not 
entirely)

interpretationoftheformalism,andcertainlyametaphysicalinterpretation 
(asisalso,Ifreelyadmit,thecommonsenseviewofthepinboard,ormy 
propensityinterpretation). 


Buttherearefurtherdifficultiesraisedbythetimereversibilitysituation. 
Oneisthis.TheSchrödingerwaveis,formally,ofadeterministiccharacter;it 
becomes 
probabilistic 
only 
through 
our 
interpretation 
(the 
Born 
interpretation,whichcorrespondstothecommonsenseinterpretationofthe 
pinboardworld).OnepointEverettmentionsinsupportofhismany-worlds 



interpretation 
is

it 
preserves 
the 
deterministic 
character 
of 
the 
Schrödingerwaveinterpretation:ifallthepossibilitiesthatthewaveopensup 
arerealized,thenindeedrealityispredeterminedbyanystatedescribedbya 
solutionofthewaveequation. 


Thiscanbeillustratedvividlybyanystatepreparationexperiment,say,by 
abeamofparticlesselectedbyanarrowslit. 


Eachparticleofthescatteringbeamcanbetakenasananalogueofoneof 
thesplittingEverettworlds;andthewholebeamasananalogueoftheEverett 
reality—themanyworldswhich,oneseeshere,arenotonlymany,butare 
scatteringinarandommannerrelativetoeachother. 


Nowletusinvertthedirectionoftime.Thenweseethatthemanyworlds 
ofthepastarearandomscatter,97yetsoarrangedthatwhentheyfusethey 
becomecorrelated,eventhoughtherewasnointeractionbetweenthembefore 
theirfusion. 


Imayjustaddthatifweconsiderthefusionofmemories,thesituation 


becomesclearlyabsurd,andmostdifficulttodescribe. 


Itisclearthattherealistictendencybehindthistheorycanbefulfilledina 


lessabsurdfashion.Infact,itisfulfilledbythepropensityinterpretation 


which,admittedly,isnomorededuciblefromtheformalismthanisEverett’s

But

38a[*(Added1981)Itisnowoftenassertedthatthefirstoftheserelations, 



isincorrect,invalid,orwhatnot,becausetisnotanoperator;andithasevenbeenassertedthat‘Δt’isa 
symbolwithoutmeaning(asifIcouldnotopentheshutterofmycamerafortheperiodΔt=1/100 
seconds;oremitalightsignalofthesamelengthoftime).Eventhepurelyformalargumentthattimeis 
notanoperatorisnolongervalid.(Cp.I.Prigogine,FromBeingtoBecoming,1980,pp.206–231.)But 
thereisaltogethermoretothis. 
Heisenberg’srelations(theindeterminacyoruncertaintyrelationsor,asIinterpretthem,the‘statistical 
scatterrelations’(seeL.Sc.D.,pp.215,225f.)areindeedpartoftheformalismofquantummechanics;but 
intheargumentsofHeisenbergandBohr,andespeciallyalsointheirargumentswithEinsteinas 
reportedbyHeisenberginPhysicsandBeyond,1971,pp.80f.,andbyBohrinP.A.Schilpp,ed.,Albert 
Einstein:Philosopher-Scientist,1949,pp.199–241,thederivationoftheHeisenbergformulaefromthe 



axiomsofquantummechanicsplaysnopartwhatever(noteventheirlogicalrelationtothe 
commutationrelationsbeingdiscussed).Whatisdiscussedareargumentsfromclassicalphysics, 
especiallywaveoptics,whichareusedtoshow,forexample,thatclassicalphysicsdoesnotallowusto 
measureaparticle’spositionandmomentum,ortheenergy(frequency)ofabeamoflight,andits 
temporallengthΔt,morepreciselythanHeisenberg’sformulaepermit.Inotherwords,theseformulae 
arenotdefendedbyshowingthattheyareforceduponusbyquantummechanics,andthattheyare 
derivablefromquantummechanics,buttheyaredefendedasconsequencesofclassicalphysics. 
Theonlydeviationfromthisisthatuseissometimesmadeofthe‘particlewaveduality’.Thisisused 
essentiallyinthepre-quantummechanicalform:itisassumedthatlightconsistsofphotonswhose 
density(statisticalprobability)isdeterminedbytheopticalwaveamplitudes;oruseismadeofBom’s 
interpretationofSchrddinger’sequation. 
Thisuseoftheparticle-waveduality(whichEinsteinofcourseaccepted)issometimesneededfor 
makingtheclassicalargumentconvincing.IsupposethatthisisoneofthereasonswhyBohrlaidso 
muchstressonthisduality.WhatisthereasonbehindthistreatmentoftheHeisenbergformulae?I 
supposeitisanattemptatshowingtherealityoftheindeterminacyrelations(onlyclassicalphysics 
treatsphysicalreality)andthustheneedforquantummechanics:itisajustificationofquantum 
mechanicsasunavoidableevenfromtheclassical,realisticpointofview.Moreover,itisanattemptto 
defendthenecessarilyprobabilisticcharacterofatomictheorywithclassicalmeans(andindependently 
ofthenon-commutativematrixalgebraoroperatoralgebra).]

QUANTUMTHEORYANDTHESCHISMIN 
PHYSICS 



CHAPTERI 



UNDERSTANDINGQUANTUMTHEORY 


ANDITSINTERPRETATIONS 


CRITICSoftheBohr-Heisenberginterpretationofquantumtheoryare,asa 
rule,dismissedasreactionarieswhosighforthegoodolddays—thedaysof 
classicalphysics;thedayswhentheorieswerenot‘abstract’butcouldeasily 
be 
visualized 
with 
the 
help 
of 
mechanical 
models; 
and 
the 
days 
of 
determinism.HenceIsupposeIshouldrepeatthatIardentlyadmirenew 
ideas,especiallyinphysics,includingideaswhichhavenotbeensuccessful. 
Also,Iamconvincedthatthe‘abstract’characterofanewtheoryisnothing 
againstit,andthatwhatstrikesonegenerationofphysicistsas‘abstract’may 
nolongerappearsotothenext.(Manyexamplescouldbegiventoillustrate 
this.)And,asImustemphasizeagain,Iamanindeterminist.1 


AlargepartofTheLogicofScientificDiscovery(L.Sc.D.)wasdevotedto 
theproblemsofquantumtheory.Butquantumtheoryisalsoanareainwhich 
myideashavedevelopedandchangedsomewhat;andIintendtopresentmy 
currentthinkingontheseissuesinthepagesthatfollow.2Beforegoinginto 
anydetails,Iwishbrieflytostatefivepointswhichindicatehowmypresent 
ideascomparetomyearlierideasaboutquantumtheory. 


(1)OneargumentconcerningquantumtheoryuponwhichIlaidgreatstress 
inthetextofL.Sc.D.—theimaginaryexperimentdescribedinsection77—is 
invalid,andIwishtowithdrawit.3 


(2)IbelieveneverthelessthatmyearlycriticismofHeisenberg’sandBohr’s 
interpretationofthequantumtheory—nowusuallycalledtheCopenhagen 
interpretation—isvalid.Itstandsonitsownfeet.Moreover,myown 
invalidexperimentcanbereplacedbyanotheronewhichisvalid—the 
famousimaginaryexperimentofEinstein,Podolsky,andRosen. 


(3) 
Inadditiontomyoldcriticismofthe‘Copenhageninterprettation’,I 
upholdmypositivethesisthattheproblemofinterpretingquantumtheory 

isboundupwiththatofinterpretingprobabilitytheory.Thusquantum 
theorywouldhavetobeinterpretedasastatisticaltheoryabout 
aggregates(or‘collectives’)ifprobabilitytheoryhadtobesointerpreted. 


(4)Butifprobabilitytheorycanbeinterpretedasatheoryofpropensities—in 
thesenseofVolumesIandIIofthisPostscript—thenquantumtheorycan 
alsobesointerpreted.Moreover,ifwedointerpretquantumtheoryasa 
theoryofphysicalpropensities,thenwecansolveallthosedifficulties 
whichhavegivenrisetotheCopenhageninterpretation. 


(5) 
When,intheearly‘thirties,Ifirstattackedwhatisnowcalledthe 
Copenhagen 
interpretation, 
I 
was 
concerned 
by 
the 
anti-rational 
tendenciesinherentinit.AtthattimeIsuggestedthatthesubjective 
interpretationofprobabilitywasresponsibleforthis:indeedthesubjective 
interpretationinducesitsaddictstotaketothatevenmoredangerous 
(becauseevenmorenarcotic)intellectualdrug,themixtureofthe 
subjectiveandobjectiveinterpretationsofprobability.Ihavelearneda 
greatdealsince,andIhavechangedmymindonafundamentalpoint(see 
VolumeI,Part2),bygivingupthefrequencyforthepropensity 
interpretation.(Bothoftheseareobjectiveinterpretations.)ButIammore 
firmlyconvincedthaneverthatthelethalmixtureofsubjectiveand 
objectiveinterpretationsofprobabilityhascreatedallthoseirrationalist 
symptoms,suchasthedreamofthequantumtheoreticalinterferenceof 
thesubjectwiththeobjectofknowledge. 
WhatIdidnotseetwentyyearsagowasthecloseconnectionbetweenthis 
mix-upandthedeterministinterpretationofclassicalphysics.4Inthelast 
analysisitmaywellturnoutthattheunconsciousdeterministprejudiceis 
chieflytobeblamedforthesubjectivetheoryofprobabilityandits 
consequence—theinvasionofmysticismintophysics. 


1ASchisminPhysics 


Sincetheautumnof1934,whenIfirstpublishedTheLogicofScientific 
Discovery(L.Sc.D.),therehavebeenmanydiscussionsoftheproblemof 
interpretingtheformalismofquantumtheory.YetIdonotthinkthatmuch 



progresshasbeenachieved,apartfromafundamentallyimportantpaperby 
Einstein,Podolsky,andRosen,whichwillbeexaminedinsection17below. 


Oneremarkableaspectofthesediscussionswasthedevelopmentofasplit 
inphysics.Somethingemergedwhichmaybefairlydescribedasaquantum 
orthodoxy:akindofparty,orschool,orgroup,ledbyNielsBohr,withthe 
veryactivesupportofHeisenbergandPauli;lessactivesympathizerswere 
MaxBornandP.JordanandperhapsevenDirac.Inotherwords,allthe 
greatestnamesinatomictheorybelongedtoit,excepttwogreatmenwho 
stronglyandconsistentlydissented:AlbertEinsteinandErwinSchrödinger. 
Foratime,afterproposinghisunorthodoxtheoryofpilotwaves,Louisde 
Broglieseemedtohavejoinedtheorthodoxy;buthelaterdissentedagain, 
encouragedbyDavidBohm’srediscoveryofthesepilotwaves.AndAlfred 
Landé,whoformanyyearssubscribedtoBohr’sposition,lateralsodissented 
forquitedifferentreasons;infact,merelyinconsequenceofadetermined 
efforttounderstand. 


Unliketheorthodoxy,thedissentersarefarfromunited.Notwoofthem 
agree(exceptperhapsdeBroglieandBohm). 


Thereisevenathirdgroupofphysicists—perhapsthemajority.Itconsists 
ofthosewhohaveturnedawayfromthesediscussionsbecausetheyregard 
them,rightly,asphilosophical,andbecausetheybelieve,wrongly,that 
philosophicaldiscussionsareunimportantforphysics.Tothisgroupbelong 
many 
younger 
physicists 
who 
have 
grown 
up 
in 
aperiod 
of 
overspecialization, 
and 
in 
the 
newly 
developing 
tradition 
of 
the 
cult 
of 
narrowness,andthecontemptforthenon-specialistoldergeneration(whose 
membersseemtothemquiteantediluvian):atraditionwhichmayeasilylead 
totheendofscienceanditsreplacementbytechnology. 


Whatisthesignificanceofthisdissension?Therearetwoclosely 
connectedissues.‘…Allphysicistsagree’,writesPauli,1‘thatthepresent 
quantumtheory,whichisinsufficienttoexplaintheatomisticnatureof 
electricityand…themassvaluesofthe“elementary”particles…canonly 
havealimitedrangeofapplication.’Inotherwords,allphysicistsagreethata 
newtheoryisneeded,inspiteofthemarvelloussuccessofthequantum 
theory.Someadmititgrudgingly,whilesomedissenterspointoutthatthe 



presenttheoryis,infact,refutedatanumberofdifferentpoints,thoughnone 
oftherefutationsisunambiguous.(Refutationsarerarelyquiteunambiguous: 
wemayremembertheperihelionmotionofMercury.)Somedissentersfeel 
thatthosewhobelongtotheorthodoxyareinclinedtobelittlethesignificance 
ofthesepoints,ortocoverthemupbyre-interpretation.Thisisoneofthe 
issues. 


2.TheSignificanceofInterpretations. 


Thesecondissueisperhapsmoreimportant.Sinceallphysicists,asPaulisays, 
hopeforareformofthetheory,thereistheproblemofhowtosearchforit. 
Thereare,inthemain,twomethods.Oneistolookatthemathematical 
formalismandtrytoalterit,andifpossibletogeneralizeit.Theotheristotry 
tointerprettheformalism,tounderstanditphysically,inthehopeofgaining 
abetterunderstandingofitsphysicaldifficultiesandshortcomings. 


Thesetwomethods(iftheymaybesocalled)arenotnecessarilyin 
conflict.Yetthemainissueatstakeisdoubtlesstheissuebetweenthem.For 
theorthodoxyhasadoptedaviewwhichiscompatibleonlywiththefirst 
method:tinkeringwiththeformalism.Asopposedtothis,everyoneofthe 
dissentershasproposednewphysicalinterpretationsinanattemptto 
understandthephysicalsituation. 


Ifwewishtolookmorecloselyatthislastand,Ibelieve,mostimportant 
issue—theproblemofunderstandingourtheories—thenwemaydiscernthat 
theorthodoxpartyrepresents,initsattitudetowardsquantumtheory,a 
philosophicaltheoryofthenatureofsciencewhichimpliesthefutilityofthe 
dissenters’attempttounderstand.Itistheviewthatthereisnothingthereto 
beunderstood:thatwecandonomorethanmasterthemathematical 
formalism,andlearnhow.toapplyit.Thisview,whichBohrandother 
membersoftheorthodoxyhavelabouredtoestablish,ispracticallytakenfor 
grantednowadaysbythethirdgroup,towhichmostoftheyoungerand 
tougherphysicistsbelong.Ihavecalledthisview‘instrumentalism’,or,more 
fully,‘theinstrumentalistinterpretationofscientifictheories’.1Itistheview 
thatall,orsome,scientifictheoriesarenothingbutmathematicalformalisms 
whichhaveusefulapplications,inparticularforthepredictionoftheresults 



ofexperiments.Theemphasisishereon‘nothingbut’;foreverybody,even 
themostardentrealist,admitsofcoursethattheoriesarealsoformalisms,and 
that 
they 
are 
marvellous 
instruments 
for 
predictions 
and 
for 
other 
applications. 


Thustheinstrumentalistviewiswidelytakenforgrantedtoday;butits 
victorywaspreparedbyBohr’sinterpretationofthequantumtheory. 


Not 
everybody 
is 
satisfied 
with 
this 
situation. 
It 
is 
especially 
unsatisfactorytothosewhoseeinphysicsnotmainlyaninstrumentfor 
predictions,orotherpracticalapplications,butratheraninstrument(if 
instrumentitmustbe)forunderstandingtheworldwelivein—forexplaining 
thisworld.Thisbecameveryclearin,forexample,thelongdiscussion 
betweenBohrandEinstein,lastingfrom1927to1949,2andcontinuedby 
Einsteinuntil1953.3RealistssuchasEinsteinandSchrödingerfeelthat 
theoriesarenotonlyinstrumentsbutarealsoattemptstodescribeaphysical 
reality.‘I(believe)inaworldofsomethingobjectivelyexistingwhichItryto 
catchinawildlyspeculativeway’,EinsteinoncewrotetoBorn.4Ofcourse 
Einsteinbelievedthathis‘wildlyspeculative’theoriesshouldbeseverely 
tested,andforthisreason,theyhadtobeinstrumentsforprediction.Butwhat 
hewasafter(thoughheknewhewouldhardlycatchit)wastherealworld,of 
whichhehopedtogiveatruedescription. 


Nothingiseasierthantodenouncestatementsofthiskindasmetaphysical 
orphilosophicalormeaningless.‘Whatwewantarefactsandfigures’,we 
hear 
from 
all 
sides, 
‘not 
philosophical 
discussions.’ 
Some 
hard-boiled 
physicistsofthepresentgenerationseemtohavenointerestinsuch 
discussions;orelsetheyleavethemtoNielsBohr.Whattheyareinterestedin 
are(i)aformalismand(ii)itsapplication;andtheyareinterestedinnothing 
else. 


Butthisinstrumentalism,thisfashionableattitudeofbeingtoughandnot 
standinganynonsense—especiallyanyphilosophicalnonsense—isitselfanold 
philosophicaltheory,howevermodernitmayseemtous.Foralongtimethe 
Churchusedtheinstrumentalistviewofscienceasaweaponagainstarising 
science.‘Youmaybeabletoforgeinstruments,gadgets,butyoucannotbythe 
lightofyourownreasondiscoveranyofthehiddensecretsofthisworld’: 



this,inbrief,wastheargumentwithwhichCardinalBellarminoopposed 
Galileo’steachingoftheCopernicansystem,andwithwhichBishopBerkeley 
opposedNewton. 


Ihavetoldalittleofthisstoryinanotherplace,5whereIalsotriedtoshow 
thattheinstrumentalistphilosophyisacreedliabletobeusedinadefensive 
mood—inanattempttoescaperefutations.Foraninstrumentraisesnoclaim 
totruth,andsocannotbefalsifiedinthesenseinwhichatheorywhichdoes 
raisesuchclaimscanbefalsified. 


Thusinstrumentalismonlyrevivesaphilosophyofconsiderableantiquity. 
But 
modern 
instrumentalists 
are, 
of 
course, 
unaware 
that 
they 
are 
philosophizing.Accordingly,theyareunawareofeventhepossibilitythat 
theirfashionablephilosophymayinfactbeuncritical,irrational,and 
objectionable—asIamconvinceditis. 


Ihavenointention,however,ofdiscussingquantumtheory,ortheschism 
inphysics,inthelightoftheproblemofrealismvs.instrumentalism.First,I 
havedealtwiththisaspectelsewhere.Secondly,Iwishtotreattheproblem 
hereunderadifferentaspect—thatofprobabilitytheory.Thesetwoaspects 
are,ofcourse,related.Aconcernwithphysicalinterpretation,asopposedto 
tinkeringwithformulae,leadstoanattackontheproblemofanobjective, 
andevenaphysical,interpretationofprobability. 


Inordernottobemisunderstood,ImustmakeclearwhatIdonotmean 
byphysicalunderstanding,andbyaphysicalinterpretation.Icertainlydonot 
meanbyitthebuildingofmodels,andleastofallthebuildingofmodelsin 
termsofasupersededtheory(suchasmechanicalmodels).NordoImean 
workingwithpicturesormetaphorswhichmaygiveusafeelingoffamiliarity 
orease.WeshouldrememberthatforceswerenoteasilyintuitedbyNewton 
andhiscontemporaries,andthatforalongtimephysicistsfoundFaraday’s 
andMaxwell’sfieldsofforcesverydifficulttounderstand.Itisnotintuitive

3.SubjectiveProbabilities,StatisticalProbabilities,andDeterminism.

4.TheObjectivityofStatisticalMechanics. 


Statisticalmechanicsprovidemanystrikingillustrationsofhowprobability

Considerabigevacuatedvacuumflaskintowhichweputalittlemedicine

Wealsoknowthatevenifwewaitforaverylongtime,weshallnever

Thissimplefacthasgivenrisetointerminablediscussionsmainlybecause

Sincethephysicalpossibilityofthiseventcannotbedoubted,weexplain

Anobjectiveprobabilisticexplanationofirreversibleprocessesofthekind 
describedisclearlyoutlinedbyPlanck.Heindicatedthat‘theoverwhelming 
majorityofcases’,ormoreprecisely,ofallpossibleinitialstates(whereeach 
‘initialstate’comprisesalltheinitialconditionsforallthemolecules)wasof 
suchakindthat‘theeventsresultingfromthemagreecompletelyintheir

noticeabledeviations[fromthementionedmeanvalues]occuronlyin…

veryspecialways…’.1Thatistosay,theveryspecialinitialconditionswhich 
wouldproducenoticeabledeviationsfromnormalbehaviourhaveavanishing 
probability. 


Themethodbywhichwecanshowthatthemeasureofthesetofinitial

states 
which

states 
equals 
zero,

almostallsufficientlylongsequences(theaverageswillbeverynearlyequal 
to½),sowecandeduce,fromsimpleassumptionsconcerningequiprobability 
ofpossiblestates,thatalmostallstateswill‘agreecompletelyintheirmean 



values’,asPlanckputsit,i.e.,thattheyhavestableaverageswithrespectto

Wemayattachthelabel‘normal’or‘approximatelyinequilibrium’toany

Inthiswaywefindthatthereisaprobabilityindistinguishablefromzero

Alltheseconsiderationsoperate,ofcourse,withastrictlyobjective 
(frequencyorpropensity)theoryofprobability:theyareasobjectiveasthe

5.TheSubjectivistInterpretationofStatisticalMechanics. 


Such,however,isnottheusualviewofthematter.Asindicatedinsection3,

WhatItaketobethecorrectviewofzeroandmaximumentropyisthis.A

Thedescriptionispreciselyanalogoustothedescriptionofarandom

Havinginthiswaydefinedmaximumrandomnessinthecaseofa

Theanalogywiththestateofagasisobvious.ThusIhaveshownthatwe

SofarIhavenotmentionedprobability.Probabilitymaycomeinnow,

Theoverwhelmingmajorityofallotherexperimentswillhaveatendency

Itshouldbenotedthatthisanalysisofrandomstatesandrandom

Themistakemayalsobedescribedthus:weareinterestedinthestructural

(Ortouseananalogousexample,wemaysaythatanyparticular

Thebeliefthatanyparticulargivenstateorsequencehaszeroentropyis

But,aswehaveseen,ithasbeenusedinthesubjectivistversion.Inthisform,

Letusnowreturntoourmedicinebottlefilledwithair;andletusassume

However,fromtheobjectivepointofview,thesystemis(mostprobably) 
inastateofdisorderorrandomness:itsentropywillbemaximal. 


Whoisright?Fromtheobjectivistpointofview,wecaneasilyfindout. 
For

Allthisreasoningis,Ibelieve,consistentwiththatofBoltzmann,who

Nowletushearthesubjectivist.Tohim,thisreasoningshouldmean 
nothing.Hehasreplacedtheterm‘order’bytheterm‘knowledge’,andthe

ThisincreaseofnesciencedoesshowacertainsimilaritytoBoltzmann’s

Thefundamentalpointisthatnesciencealwaysincreases,providedwedo 
notstartwithcompleteknowledge.Butdisorder,orentropy,decreasesat

evidenceofthese‘fluctuations’intheso-calledBrownianmovementsince 
Einstein’sinterpretationof1905.Soitdoesnotseempossibletomaintainthe 
subjectivistidentificationofdisorder(orentropy)andnescience. 


Secondly,nesciencedoesnotincreaseifwehavecompleteorperfect 
knowledgetostartwith(asbothvonNeumannandBornimply);forperfect 
knowledgeallowsustocalculateallfuturestateswithperfectprecision.Again 
thisisincompatiblewithBoltzmann’sview;forifeverasystemshouldattain 
perfectorderbyahighlyimprobablefluctuation,itwould,inallprobability, 
immediatelybecomedisorderedagain,accordingtoBoltzmann. 


Thusthe 
often 
repeatedclaim 
that 
wecan, 
as 
it 
were, 
translate 
Boltzmann’stheoryintosubjectivelanguagemustbedismissed. 


HavingtriedtoexplainwhatItaketobethecorrectview,Inowturn,as 
promised,totherefutationofthesubjectivisttheoryofentropyordisorder.I 
shallattemptverybrieflytoshowthatthetheoryisabsurd. 


Letusagainopenourmedicinebottleinthevacuumflask.Whatwewish 
toexplainiswhythegaswillalwaysescape,andwhyitescapesirreversibly. 
Bothobjectivistsandsubjectivistsexplainitasaresultofthelawthatentropy 
ordisordertendstoincrease.Theobjectivistwillpointoutthatinthevolume 
nowavailable,thepositionsofthemoleculeswouldbehighlyorderedifthe 
gasweretoremaininthebottle,andthattherewillbeanoverwhelming 
probability,inthesenseofanoverwhelmingpropensity,forthegastoassume 



astateinwhichthepositionsofthemoleculesaredistributedinarandom 
fashionovertheavailablespace.Thustheobjectivistcanofferanexplanation 
(nevermindwhetheritisentirelyadequate)ofwhythegasescapes. 


Thesubjectivist,however,cannotofferanysuchexplanation.Hecannot 
evensaythatthegashasinfactexpanded.Allhecansayisthathisstateof 
ignorancehasincreased(providedhedidnotstartwithperfectknowledge). 
Thisisabsurd.Forwhatwewantedtoexplainwasnotthestateofignorance, 
butthestateofthegas;notthatknowledgehasbeenlost,butthatgashas 
beenlostfromthemedicinebottle. 


‘Thetimevariationsofentropy’,vonNeumannsumsup,‘arethenbased 
onthefactthattheobserverdoesnotknoweverything.’7aThusiftheobserver 
knewourbottlefulcompletely,theentropywouldnotincrease.Therewould 
betwopossibilities:(i)thegaswouldstayinthebottle,or(ii)thegaswould 
escape,(i)isclearlyabsurd(itwouldmeanamysticinfluenceofour 
knowledgeuponthestateofthegas).Butif(ii)thegasescapes,itsescape 
cannotbeconnectedwithanincreaseofentropy.Indeed,ourperfect 
knowledgewouldallowustopredicttheescapewithoutmakinguseofthe 
lawofentropyincrease:wecouldpredicttheescapebycalculatingthetracks 
ofallthemolecules.Butinspiteofthefactthatwedonotneedtoappealto 
theentropylaw,itisabsurdtodenythattheescapeproceedsinaccordance 
withit:thegashasescaped,eventhough,inthisparticularcase,knowledge 
hasnotescapedwithit.Anditwastheescapeofthegaswhichwasfirst 
describedasanincreaseofentropy. 


Fromadeterministpointofview,itisindeeddifficulttoseewhat 
statisticalmechanicsisfor:why,ifallpredictivequestionsaboutgases 
escapingfromallsinglebottleswithgiveninitialconditionshavebeen 
answered,aquestionshouldremain;thequestion,Imean,ofthelaw-like 
character,theregularityofthegasescapes,orofthemixingprocesses,andthe 
questionoftheirreversibilityoftheseprocesses.Theseregularitiescould 
remainunnoticed,evenifwecouldineverysinglecasepredictpreciselywhat 
willhappen. 


While 
Isee 
in 
statistical 
mechanics 
an 
attempt 
to 
explain 
these 
regularities,vonNeumanndescribes8thestatisticaltreatmentas‘aluxuryof 



extraaddition’whichbecomesimportantonlyifourknowledgeisdeficient.In 
thisattitudeliesthesourceofthewholetrouble:thesubjectivetheoryagain 
showsitselfasaremnantofadeterministprejudice. 


6.OscillationsBetweentheTwoInterpretations. 


ThatPauliwaversbetweenthetwointerpretationshasalreadybeenindicated: 
hereadsCantelli’sstronglawinanobjectivistsense.ButvonNeumann’sand 
Born’sinterpretationsalsooscillate.ThuswefindinvonNeumann’sbook1a 
passageinwhichvonMises’stheoryof‘collectives’isdescribedas‘ingeneral 
necessaryforestablishingprobabilitytheoryasatheoryoffrequencies’;andit 
isimpliedthattheauthoracceptsthistheory.ButvonMises’sviewsare 
utterlyincompatiblewithanysubjectivetheory,asvonMisesalways 
emphasized;andvonNeumannmakesnoattempttotellushowthetwo 
viewscanpossiblybecombined. 


SimilarlyMaxBorn.Ihavementionedhissubjectivism;buttherearealso 
objectivistpassagesinBorn’swork.‘Irreversibilitycanbeunderstoodonlyby 
explicitlyexemptingpartofthesystemfromcausality’isapassageofthis 
kind.2(Ifeelinclinedtoagreewiththespiritofthepassage,eventhoughIdo 
notagreewithitswording.)Inthecontinuationofthispassage,Born’s 
waveringseemsimplicit.‘Onehastoabandonthecondition’,hewrites,‘… 
thatthepositionsandvelocitiesoftheparticlesareundercontrol….’Theterm 
‘undercontrol’,whichisnotexplained,seemstometoindicateawavering 
between‘arenotknown’—whichwouldbeinkeepingwithmostofhistext— 
and‘cannotbereproducedatwillifwerepeattheexperiment’,orinother 
words‘arenotpartof(orperhaps,‘cannotbemadepartof)‘theobjective 
conditionsoftheexperiment’—whichwouldindicateaninclinationtowards 
anobjectiveinterpretation. 


Suchwaveringoroscillatingisindeedanecessaryconsequenceofany 
concessiontosubjectivism.ForIdonotbelieveforaminutethattheauthors 
citedreallyintendedtoexplainirreversiblephysicalprocesses,suchasmixing, 
ordiffusion,byourignorance.Isupposethat,facedwiththeseconsequences, 
theywouldwithdrawthestatementsquoted,oratleasttrytore-interpret 
them.ButIfeelcertaintheytookupthesepositionsbecausetheywereforced 



intothembyrealdifficultiesinherentintheirfundamentalapproach.

CHAPTERII 



THEOBJECTIVITYOFQUANTUM 
THEORY 


7.TheObjectivityofQuantumTheory:TheBoxes. 


Itisoftenassertedthat,inviewofthesituationinquantumtheory,objectand 
subjectcannolongerbesharplyseparated.1TouseHeitler’swords,the 
‘separationoftheworldintoan“objectiveoutsidereality”,and“us”,theselfconsciousonlookers,
cannolongerbemaintained.Objectandsubjectbecome 
inseparablefromeachother’.2This,accordingtoBohr,isdueto‘the 
impossibilityofanysharpseparationbetweenthebehaviourofatomicobjects 
andtheinteractionwiththemeasuringinstrumentswhichservetodefinethe 
conditionsunderwhichthephenomenaappear’.3Heitlerelaboratesthepoint 
insomedetail.‘Onemayask’,hewrites,‘ifitissufficienttocarryouta 
measurementbyaself-registeringapparatusorwhetherthepresenceofan 
observerisrequired.’Andhearrivesattheconclusionthattheself-registering 
apparatusisinsufficient,andthat‘theobserverappears,asanecessarypartof 
thewholestructure,andinhisfullcapacityasaconsciousbeing’.4 


WemustbegratefultoHeitlerforgivinguswhatisperhapsthesharpest 
formulationofthisdoctrineoftheinvolvementofthesubjectwiththephysical 
object;butitalsofrequentlyoccurs,inoneformorother,inHeisenberg’s 
PhysicalPrinciplesoftheQuantumTheory,andinmanyotherstatementsof 


theorthodoxview. 
Butthisdoctrineissimplyfalse:quantumtheoryisasobjectiveasany 
theorycanbe. 
TakingupHeitler’schallenge,wefirstmaydescribeaclassicalself


registeringexperimentasfollows:aboxispreparedinwhichtheexperimental 
arrangementissetup,includingaself-registeringapparatus.Thentheboxis 
closed.Thereadingoftheapparatus(withincertainlimitswhichmaybevery 
narrow)ispredictedbythetheory.Afteratime,theboxissuesatapeorfilm 
onwhichthereadingisrecorded;andwecompareitwiththepredicted 



reading. 


Nowthesituationinquantumtheoryisinprincipleexactlythesame,with 
onlyonecharacteristicdifference:foreverytypicalquantumtheoretical 
experimentweneed,insteadofonebox,abatteryofmanyboxes,eachfitted 
withthesameexperimentalset-up—orwithset-upsassimilaraswecanmake 
them;andthetheorypredicts,inmostcases,nottherecordingofanysingle 
tapeorfilmissuedbyeachoftheboxes,butthestatisticaldistributionofall 
thesereadingsproducedbyalltheseboxes. 


Insomespecialcases,thetheoryevenmakescertainpredictions,i.e., 
predictionsabouteverysinglereadingissuedbyeverysinglebox.For 
example,iftheexperimentisonewithadilutedbeamofelectrons,andthe 
recordingapparatusconsistsoftwofilms,placedinlayerfashionontopof 
eachother,whichretain‘animageofthepointwheretheelectron(orthe 
electrons)passedthrough’,5thenweshallalwaysfindthattheimagesonthe 
twofilmsverynearlycoincide.Wecanthus‘predictwithcertaintytheresult 
ofthisobservation’—indirectoppositiontotheassertionofHeitler6whose 
exampleisidenticaltoours,butwhoarrivesattheopposite(and,Iamafraid, 
‘evidentlyimpossible’)result;whichshowsthatevenaphysicistofthefirst 
rankcanbemisledbythequantumorthodoxy. 


InHeitler’sparticularexamplewedonotneedmorethanonebox;thisis 
duetothefactthattheresultinquestioncanbepredictedwithprobability1 
(‘withcertainty’).Butforthemoretypicalquantumexperiments,abatteryof 
boxesisneeded,orabatteryofrepetitionsoftheexperimentintheonebox. 


Nowhereintheliteraturedowefindanexperimentwhichcannotbe 
rearranged,bythemethodexplainedhere,in‘aself-registeringapparatus’,or 
inabatteryofapparatuses—indirectcontradictiontoHeitler’sassertion. 
Quantumtheoryisexactlyasobjectiveasanyotherphysicaltheory.7 


8.TheSourceoftheConfusion:the‘ReductionoftheWavePacket’. 


Whythenisthetheorysooftenmisinterpretedinasubjectivistsense?Why 
doesHeitlerbelievethattheobserverplaysanessentialpartintheexperiment 
inwhichadilutedelectronbeamimpingesupontwofilmsplacedinlayer 
fashionontopofoneanother? 



Theanswerisperfectlyclear:themathematicalformalism—thatistosay, 
Schrödinger’swaveequation—gives,combinedwithBorn’sinterpretation, 
onlytheprobability(density)distributionoftheelectronswhichimpinge 
uponthefilms.Itdoesnottellusthattheplaceswheretheelectronsimpinge 
uponthefirstandthesecondfilmwillcoincide. 


However,thislatterresultcanbeobtainedifwetreattheplaceofthe 
impactoftheelectronuponthefirstfilmasanewinitialconditionforanew 
applicationofSchrödinger’sequation. 


The 
‘observer’—or 
indeed, 
the 
physicist 
equipped 
not 
only 
with 
observational 
results, 
but 
with 
Schrödinger’s 
equation—seems 
to 
enter 
explicitlybecausethemathematicalformalismdoesnotyieldinonestepthe 
predictionoftheresultofthetwo-filmexperiment.Twoapplicationsofthe 
formalismareneeded,andinordertoapplytheequationforthesecondtime, 
anobservationseemstobeneeded. 


Thisis,inbrief,theargumentofHeitler(andoftheorthodoxschool).But 
insofarasitassertsthatanobservermustinterveneinordertomakethe 
secondsteppossible—theso-called‘reductionofthewavepacket’(tothepoint 
ofthefirstimpact)—wheneverweapplySchrödinger’sequationtoanycoordinatesofanimpactonthefirstfilm,
itwillleadtothepredictionthatthe 
impactonthesecondfilmwillhave(verynearly)thesameco-ordinates.No 
observationisneededforthisresult—neitheran‘actual’nora‘potential’one. 


Thisiswhytheargumentfails,andwhyourself-registeringboxeswork, 
inthesensethatwecanmakesuccessfulpredictions,notonlyforthestatistics 
ofthoseresultswhichexhibitscattering,butalsoforthoseresults—likethe 
coincidenceonourtwosuperimposedfilms—whichdonotshowanyfurther 
scatter. 


Theorthodoxargumentdoesestablish,however,thoughunintentionally, 
theincompletenessofthemathematicalformalism. 


9.TheIncompletenessoftheMathematicalFormalismofQuantumTheory. 


Therearemanysensesinwhichonecanspeakofthecompletenessor 
incompletenessofatheory,orofamathematicalformalism.Theideaofthe 
completenessofamathematicalformalismwhichIshallusehereisintended 



tocoveracertainrangeofphysicalphenomenainthefollowingsense:the 
formalismiscompleteifandonlyifitcovers‘automatically’(i.e.,withoutthe 
needtomakeanon-formal‘secondstep’,inthesenseofthepreceding 
section)anypredictionofexperimentalresultswhichwecanmakeinthe 
rangeofphenomenainquestion. 


Theproposaltousetheterm‘complete’inthissensecomesfromNiels 
Bohr.InhisreplytoapaperbyEinstein,Podolsky,andRosenentitled‘Can 
Quantum-Mechanical 
Description 
of 
Physical 
Reality 
be 
Considered 
Complete?’1,Bohrwrote:‘Suchanargumentation,however,wouldhardly 
seemsuitedtoaffectthesoundnessofquantummechanicaldescription,which 
isbasedonacoherentmathematicalformalismcoveringautomaticallyany 
procedureofmeasurementlikethatindicated.’2Sincethe‘soundness’in 
questionwastheproblemofcompleteness,andsincebya‘procedureof 
measurement’,coveredbytheformalism,Bohrclearlymeansexperimental 
resultspredictablebytheformalism,onemightsaythatthedefinitionof 
‘complete’heresuggestedis,asnearasitcanbe,Bohr’s. 


Butaccordingtothisdefinition,theformalismisincomplete—indirect 
oppositiontoBohr’scontention.Infacttheorthodoxschooltriestohaveit 
bothways. 


Bohrsaystheformalismcoversallcasesautomatically.ButHeitlersays 
thattheobservermustintervene;orasHeisenbergputthesamepoint:‘The 
discontinuous“reductionofthewavepackets”whichcannotbederivedfrom 
Schrüdinger’sequation,is…aconsequenceofthetransitionfromthepossible 
totheactual.’3Butitiscertainlynotpossibletoinsistontheonehandthatthe 
formalismiscompleteandtoinsistontheotherhandthatitsapplicationto 
‘theactual’actuallydemandsastepwhichcannotbederivedfromit. 


Inordernottobemisunderstood,IwishtostressthatIdonotthinkthat 
theincompletenessmentionedhereisaseriousone.Idonotevensuggestthat 
itistheonewhichEinsteinhadinmind.Ishallshowthat,inspiteofthis 
incompletenessintheformalism,thereisnoincompletenessintheinterpreted 
theory;indeed,thisparticularincompletenessintheformalismisonewhichis 
characteristicoftheprobabilistictreatmentofawiderangeofproblemswhich 
havenothingtodowithquantumphysicsorinterferencebyobserversor 



anythingofthatkind.Forthisveryreason,thereisnoneedtoremedythis 
particularincompleteness.4AllIwantedtoshowwasthattheformalismofthe 
theoryisincomplete,preciselyinBohr’ssense,asagainstBohr’sassertion; 
andthatitisjustthistypicalandharmlessincompletenesswhichhasledto 
themythoftheobserver’sentryintoquantumtheory.[Seethediscussionof 
completenessinthe‘Preface1982’.] 


10.ARandomWalkandthe‘TransitionfromthePossibletotheActual’. 


Asoldierinthedesertisgivenapocketroulette-wheelandinstructedtosetit 
up,lettingthepointerspinandcometorest,andthentomarchforone 
minuteinthedirectionindicatedbythepointer;andthentorepeatthe 
performance,lettingthepointerspinagain,andagainmarchingforone 
minuteinthedirectionofthepointer.Itisatonceintuitivelyclearthat,onthe 
basisofthisinstruction,weobtainaprobabilitydistributionforthepositionof 
thesoldierwhichspreadswiththesoldier’smarchingspeedfromthestarting 
pointinalldirections—akindofcloudwhichisdenseinthecentreandthins 
outtowardsitsperiphery.(ThereisakindofHuygensprinciplevalidforthis 
cloud:everypotentialendofoneminute’swalkbecomesthecentreofanew 
cloudwhichissuperimposedupontheothers.) 


Nowletusobservethesoldier,afteronehour,whenheisjustconsulting 
hispocketroulette-wheel.Then,wemaysay,theoldclouddisappears,anda 
newcloudisstartedatthepointatwhichwehaveobservedthesoldier. 


Thisispreciselythesamethingasthe‘reductionofthewavepacket’,and 
itoccurs,preciselyinthisway,ineveryso-calledMarkov-chainproblemof 
probabilitytheory,and,slightlymoregenerally,ineverycaseinwhich 
relativeprobabilitiesplayanessentialpart.Itevenoccurs,thoughinaslightly 
moretrivialway,inanysuchgameastossingpennies:relativetothe 
informationthatwehavetossedheads,the(relative)probabilitythatwehave 
tossedheadschangesfrom½to1.Thatthisisfundamentallythesame 
problemasthatwhichhasdisturbedthequantumtheorists(thatitistherefore 
thesameasHeitler’sproblem,andthesameasthatwhichmakesHeisenberg 
talkaboutthe‘transitionfromthepossible[orpotential]totheactual’)I 
assertedmanyyearsago,especiallyinL.Sc.D.,section76. 



Theharmlessnessofthisfamous‘reductionofthewavepacket’is,of 
course,whyIconsidertheincompletenessoftheformalismtobeharmless: 
oncewecaninterpretitproperly,theinterpretedtheory—ratherthanthe 
mathematicalformalismassuch—isinthisparticularpointascompleteaswe 
canwishittobe. 


Inallthesecasesthe‘reductionofthewavepacket’hasofcoursenothing 
whatevertodowithanallegedinterferenceoftheobserver,orofthe 
observinginstruments,withtheobjectsobserved:soldiers,andevenpennies, 
aretooheavytobeupsetbythefewlightquantaneededforobservingthem. 
Thisisfinallyadmitted,implicitly,byHeisenberg,inthepaperquoted,forhe 
nowfinds(pp.25and27)the‘transitionfromthepossibletotheactual’alsoin 
classicalthermodynamics.Butthisdestroystheorthodox(or‘Copenhagen’) 
interpretationwhichhedefendsinthatpaper.Forthewholeissueiswhether 
quantumtheoryissoverydifferentfromotherprobabilistictheoriesasthe 
orthodoxschoolalwaysasserted;whetherinquantumtheory,asopposedto 
‘classical’or‘macroscopic’theories,wearefaced,owingtothepeculiarityof 
theobjectdealtwithandowingtoPlanck’sconstanth,withanewandunique 
situation. 


11.Particles,Waves,andthePropensityInterpretation. 


Theproblemofparticlesvs.waves,andoftherelationsbetweenparticlesand 
waves,hasforaconsiderabletimebeendiscussedbysomeofthefoundersof 
quantumtheory(especiallybyBohr,Born,Schrödinger,Heisenberg,Dirac, 
andlaterbyLandé).Inthesediscussions,acertainelementofirritationor 
exasperationissometimesdiscernible,directedespeciallyagainstSchrödinger 
who,itisindicated,has‘apersonalattachmenttothewaves’,and‘isinlove 
withthisidea’.1Theirritationisperhapsevenmoremarkedamongphysicists 
oftheyoungergenerationwhofeelinclinedtolookupontheproblemasa 
philosophicalpseudo-problemratherthanaphysicalone.Foritisaproblem 
ofneither(i)themathematicalformalismnor(ii)itsapplication.Onbothof 
thesepoints,thecontestingpartiesagree.2Thustheredoesnotseemtobea 
problemherethatneedbetakenseriouslybythetougherphysicists. 


Thosetough,no-nonsenseinstrumentalistswhothinklikethisshould 



pausetoconsiderthatitwasSchrödingerwhomadethediscoveryofthe 
mathematicalequivalenceofthetwotheories;whofirstdevelopedthe 
‘transformationtheory’(ofvectorsinHilbertspace3),andwhowroteaslong 
agoas1926abouthisequivalenceproof:‘Onthisview’(thatofKirchhoffand 
Mach)‘mathematicalequivalencehasalmostthesamemeaningasphysical 
equivalence.’ThusifSchrödingerbelievesthatthetwotheories,thatofwaves 
andthatofparticles,arenotequivalentphysically,thereasonisnotafailure 
toseeapointwhich,infact,nobodysawasearlyor,Ibelieve,asclearlyashe 
did. 


Infact,Schrödingerwastoogenerouswhenheconcededasmuchas 
‘almost’physicalequivalence:hiswavetheorywasatheoryofbothfreeand 
boundelectrons,whichtheparticletheorywasnot,atleastatthetime.4 


IfSchrödinger’sattempttocriticizetheorthodoxviewledtosuchreplies, 
myviews,whicharemerelythoseofaphilosopher,havelittlehopeofbeing 
takenseriouslybyphysicists;thehopeisevenlessenedbythefactthat,

particles5)Ineverthelessbelievethatthequantumtheoryisinaverydefinite 
senseaparticletheory(hereIdisagreewithSchrödinger)andinasensewhich 
excludesaduality,oranalogy,orcomplementarity,betweenparticlesand 
waves.Tobemoreexplicit,Ibelievethatthewaves(eventhoseofthesecond 
quantization) 
are 
mathematical 
representations 
of 
propensities, 
or 
of

set-up),interpretableaspropensitiesoftheparticlestotakeupcertainstates. 
Insofarasquantumtheoryisaprobabilistictheory,itisatheoryofwaves,or 
ofmatrices:thisiswhythewavetheoryandthematrixtheoryareequivalent. 
Buttheprobabilitieswhichthetheorydeterminesarealwaysthepropensities 
ofparticlestotakeupacertainstateundercertainconditions. 


Thereisthusnosymmetryordualitybetweenparticlesandwaves:the 
wavesdescribedispositionalpropertiesoftheparticles.AsAlfredLandeputs

of 



incontrastibles.’6Ifullyagree,exceptthatIwouldhavepreferredamore 
obviouslydispositionalpropertythanwavynecks;7forexample,theswans’ 
dispositiontomake,undercertaincircumstances,wavymovementswiththeir 
necks. 


Accordingtothisview,theparticlesdonotappear,explicitly,inthe 
formalism—neitherinthewaveformalism,norinthematrixformalism 
(whichisoftensaidtobeaparticleformalism),noryetinthe‘neutral’ 
transformationtheory.Allthesetheoriesdescribethepropensitiesofcertain 
variablestotakeupcertainvalues;variableswhichmaybeinterpretedas 
variablesofthestateofcertainparticles. 


Oneofmyworriesisthatthisviewwouldnotbeacceptableto 
Schrödinger.Yetitisreallynotfarremovedfromhisownview.Onemust 
rememberthat,accordingtotheinterpretationproposedhere,propensities, 
andfieldsofpropensities,arejustasrealasforces,andfieldsofforces.They 
are,likeforces,dispositionalproperties;andlikeforces,orfieldsofforces, 
theyarepropertiesnotsomuchoftheparticlesasofthetotalphysical 
situation;theyare,likeforces,relationalproperties. 


Itmaybeworthwhiletoexplainthispointalittlemorefully.Onereason 
whyNewtonfounditdifficulttoaccepthisowntheoryasanexplanationof 
gravitywasthathefounditdifficulttoseeinhisattractingforces—actingata 
distance—aninherentoranessentialpropertyofmatter.Extensioncouldbe 
anessentialpropertyofapieceofmatter,becauseitdidnotdependupon 
anythingbesidesthispieceofmatter.Butattractionwasadispositionto 
attractotherpiecesofmatter:itwasarelationalproperty,amutualproperty; 
anditwasthereforedifficulttoconceiveitasinherentinthepieceofmatter— 
asdifficultasitistoregardasinherentthepropertyinapieceofmatterof 
beingmoreextended(bigger)thananotherpieceofmatter.8 
Itisour 
Aristotelianandessentialisthabitofexpressingourselves(connectedwitha 
habitualpreferenceforusing‘categorical’,i.e.,subject-predicatestatements) 
whichsuggeststhatrelationalpropertiesarenot‘real’but‘ideal’;thatisto 
say,thattheyarefoundedonlyinourownthinkingandorderingactivities.It 
isthisattitude,inthelastanalysis,whichleadstothesubjectiveinterpretation 
ofprobabilities,andwhichmakesitdifficulttoacceptanobjectivepropensity 



interpretation. 


Onceweallowthatmeasuresofpossibilitiesmaybeinterpretedas 
propensitiestoreactincertainwaysundercertaingivencircumstances(initial 
andboundaryconditions),ourinterpretationintermsofpossibilitiesisno

Schrödingercomments:‘Thisnewinterpretationmayshockusatfirst

Thepropensityinterpretationbridgesthisgap.Itallowsthesinglesystem

Theascriptionofanewideaofobjectiveprobabilityofthiskind—

probabilityconsiderations.Inotherwords,probabilitytheoryentersbecause

Allthisappearedsuddenlytohavebeenthrownout,andapurelyobjective

Yettherewasnocauseforalarm.Inspiteofthepassagequoted,therewas

Amongthosewhoanticipatedthepropensityinterpretation,onlyLandé

ThereisonlyonepointofimportancewhereIfeelthathedoesnotfully

13.ArethereQuantumJumps? 


ThetitleofthissectionistakenfromapaperbySchrödinger1inwhich,I 
believe,heshowsthatquantumtheorydoesnotwarrantaninterpretation 
accordingtowhichtherearequantumjumps. 


QuantumjumpswerefirstintroducedbyBohrwithhisatommodelof 
1913.Theyweresuddentransitionsofelectronsfromonestationarystateto 
another,transitionswhichwerethensaidnottotakeupanytime.However, 
theemissionofalightquantum(whichthetheoryconnectswiththe 
transition)doestakeuptime,accordingbothtothenewquantumtheoryand 
toexperiments.Fromthepointofviewofthetheory,thefactthatthe 
emissiontakestimecanbeseen,forexample,fromtheindeterminacy 
relations,sinceotherwisethefrequency(energy)ofthequantumwouldhave 
tobeindeterminate.Fromthepointofviewofexperiment,theaveragetime 
canbecalculated,forexample,fromtheaveragelengthofacoherentwave 
train(3to4feetinlength)2. 


QuantumjumpsareanoldaversionofSchrödinger’s.PersonallyIdonot 
feelanydistasteforthem—Ishouldbeperfectlyhappytoacceptthemif 
physicistscouldshowgoodreasonsfordoingso.ButInolongerbelievethat 
therearesuchreasons;atleastnonearegiveninthetwo(somewhat 
contradictory)replieswhichSchrödinger’spaperhasreceived,onefromMax 
Born,3andalateronefromHeisenberg.4Born,sofarasIcansee,doesnot 
answerSchrödinger’squestionatall.Instead,hediscussesacompletely 
differentquestionwhichheintroducesinthetitleofhissection2:‘Arethere 
Atoms?.’HeclaimsthatSchrödinger’srealintentionistodenythatatoms 
exist.ThisseemstobeaninexplicablemisinterpretationofSchrödinger’s 
views,andmoreespeciallyofwhathesaysintheplacecriticizedbyBorn. 
Schrödinger’stheorymayexplainatoms(notaswaves,butaswave 
structures);butitdoesnotexplainthemaway. 


WhilenoanswertoSchrödinger’squestionisgiveninBorn’spaper,5 
Heisenbergdoesgiveananswer.Atfirst,hedoesnotseemtosaymorethan 
Born,forheappeals(insteadoftotheexistenceofatoms)totheexistenceof 
an‘elementofdiscontinuity…whichisfoundeverywhereinatomicphysics 



(veryobviously,forinstanceonthescintillationscreen)’.6Thisclearlydoes 
notcarryusonestepbeyondBorn’sposition.Thatscintillationsarenewsto 
Schrödingerisnewstome;butitreallyseemsthatitisnolongergenerally 
knownthatSchrödingerneverdeniedthesediscontinuities,butthathetried 
toexplainthem—andnotentirelywithoutsuccess.Butafterthis,Heisenberg 
makesadefinitesuggestionconcerningourquestion;hesays:‘Intheusual 
interpretationofquantumtheoryit’(‘it’isthediscontinuity,andpresumably 
thequantumjump)‘iscontainedinthetransitionfromthepossibletothe 
actual.’HavingthusmadehisowncounterproposaltoSchrödinger’sproposal 
toabandonthemythofquantumjumps,Heisenbergconcludeshiscriticismof 
Schrödinger—ofthemanwhoexplainedstationarystatesandquantum 
numbers—bysaying:‘Schrödingerhimselfmakesnocounterproposalasto 
howheintendstointroducetheelementofdiscontinuity,everywhere 
observable,inadifferentmannerfromtheusualinterpretation.’(Were 
eigenvaluesnoproposal?) 


ThusSchrödingerreceivestwoanswers:thefirstallegesthathisdenialof 
quantumjumpsisequivalenttoadenialofatomsorother‘discontinuities’; 
andthesecondsuggeststhatthequantumjumpsareidenticalwiththe 
famous,orrathernotorious,‘transitionfromthepossibletotheactual’—thatis 
tosay,withthe‘reductionofthewavepacket’. 


Idiscussedthisreductionlongago,inL.Sc.D.,section75,andhavenow 
donesoagain,insection8ofthisvolume.Nodoubt,thereductionofawave 
packetcanhappenverysuddenly;evenwithsuper-luminalvelocity,asI 
explainedinsection75ofL.Sc.D.;foritsimplyisnotaphysicalevent—itisthe 
resultofthefreechoiceofnewinitialconditions(orofusinganewbin‘p(a, 
b)’). 


WhatismostremarkableisthatHeisenberghimself,inthesamepaper, 
admitsthatthis‘reduction’isnotaquantumeffect;forhesays(p.27)thatitis 
‘exactlyasinGibbs’thermodynamics,aconsequenceofthetransitionfrom 
thepotentialtotheactual’.ThushisanswertoSchrödingeramountstothe 
assertionthattherearequantumjumps—exactlyastherearequantumjumps 
intheclassicaltheory. 


Theseanswersstronglysuggestthattherereallyappearstobenoevidence 



forquantumjumps.Butifthisisso,whynotsayso?Andwhyattack 
Schrödingerwhenhesaysso? 


Ifwenowlookattheproblemfromthepointofviewofthepropensity 
interpretation,itbecomes,perhaps,alittlecleareratonepoint.First,itisclear 
thattherecannotbequantumjumpsintheoldsense,becauseofthe 
indeterminacyprinciple(withrespecttotimeandenergy).Secondly,the 
‘reductionofthewavepacket’iscertainlynoquantumjump,whatever 
Heisenbergmaysay;forotherwisesoldiersandpennieswouldmakequantum 
jumps.Thirdly—andthisistheresultofthepropensityinterpretation— 
quantumtheoryisnotatheorywhichdescribesdynamicalprocessesintime, 
butitisaprobabilisticpropensitytheorywhichgivesweighttovarious 
possibilities.Thusevenwhereitdoesnotimplythataprocesstakestime,the 
processmayneverthelesstaketime.Ihaveinmindthecaseofinteraction 
betweenaparticleandapolarizer;itappearsthattheparticle‘jumps’,in 
consequenceofthisinteraction,intooneoftwostates,becomingpolarized 
eitherperpendicularorparalleltotheopticalaxisofthepolarizer.Thisis 
suggested,notonlybyDirac,7forexample,butalsobyLandé.8 


Nowthismaybetrue,forallIknow(althoughIdonotbelievethatitis). 
Butitseemstomefairlyclearthatnobodyknowsittobetrue,oreventobea 
consequence 
of 
the 
theory. 
All 
the 
theory 
tells 
us—in 
its 
propensity 
interpretation—isthattherearecertainpropensitiesfortheparticlestotakeup 
oneortheotheroftwostatesuponinteraction.Iteventellsusthat 
comparableprocessesdotaketime(suchasinteractionwithalight-absorbing 
filter,forreasonsanalogoustothoseconnectedwiththeemissionofalight 
quantum).Thusitseemstomeclearthatinthiscasetoo,thetheorydoesnot 
implythattherearequantumjumps;andIthinkthisiswhatSchrödingersaid; 
thoughhenotonlysaiditverymuchbetter,butmademanyothermost 
interestingsuggestionsforthefurtherdevelopmentofthetheory. 


14.ArethereParticles? 


WehaveseenthatBornandHeisenbergcounterSchrödinger’spertinent 
question‘Aretherequantumjumps?’bysuggestingthatthequestionwhich 
Schrödingerreallywantedtoaskwas,‘Arethereatoms?’or‘Arethere 



discontinuities?’.Ihavetriedtoshowthatthesesuggestionsaresomewhat 
evasive,andhardlyfairtoSchrödinger. 


Butthereisnoreasonwhythesuggestedquestionshouldnotbetakenup. 
Arethereparticles?Arethereatoms,electrons,photons,nucleons,andthe 
hostofotherparticleswhichhavebeendiscoveredduringthelasttwenty-five 
years?Theanswerhastobeaqualified‘yes’. 


Tomakeclearwhatismeantbyaqualified‘yes’,wehavefirstto 
distinguishtwodifferentquestions.Thefirstisthequestionwhetherone 
scientistoranotherbelieves,orconjectures,thatthereareparticles.The 
secondiswhethertheexistenceofparticlesisimpliedintheinterpretationof 
thepresentquantumtheoreticalformalism. 


TothefirstquestiontheansweristhatSchrödingernodoubtbelievesin 
particles,butthathewasthefirstamongthequantumtheoriststoseeboth 
thepossibilityofexplainingparticlesbysomethingelse,andtheneedforsuch 
anexplanation.Hedidsoatatimewhenthe‘elementaryparticles’,asthe 
electronsandprotonswereusuallycalled,werestillgenerallytakentobe 
indestructiblebricks.Today,wenolongerusetheword‘particle’withthe 
implicationthatthesethingsareindestructible.Butitshouldnotbeforgotten 
thatthefirstsuggestionthatparticlesmayhavealimitedlifecamefrom 
Schrödinger’stheory. 


Nobody,leastofallSchrödinger,wouldsuggestthathistheorywas 
successfulineveryrespect.Buthisfundamentalposition—thatparticlesare 
notultimatebutareinneedofanexplanation,intermsofsomethingother 
thanparticles—willhardlybequestioned. 


Schrödinger’stheorywasthefirstand,fundamentally,theonlyattemptin 
thisdirection,andonemightsaythatthenewmeaningoftheterm‘particle’ 
owesverymuch,ifnoteverything,tothefactthatwehavelearnedfromhis 
theoryhowtothinkintermsofparticleswhicharenotultimateor 
‘elementary’.Inotherwords,althoughitdoesnotseemthathistheory 
providesasatisfactoryexplanationofparticlesandtheirtransitions,ithas 
shownthat,andhow,anexplanationmightbegiven.Indeed,thisisallwe 
possessbywayofanapproachtotheoryinthisfield. 


AllthisshouldberememberedifwewishtoassessHeisenberg’sremark 



that‘Schrödingerhimselfmakesnocounterproposalastohowheintendsto 
introducetheelementofdiscontinuity’—forexamplethediscontinuitydueto 
theexistenceofparticles.Itshouldalsoberememberedifwewishtoassess 
Pauli’scommentsonthe‘opposition’againsttheorthodoxschool.‘Thereisno 
lackofanopposition’,Pauliwrites,‘…butithasremainedbarrenandgot 
stuckinastateofregressive[orretrograde]hope…Theonewingofthe 
opposition,towhichSchrödingerbelongs’,Paulicontinueswithmarkedirony, 
‘holdswavestobemorebeautifulthanparticlesandthereforetriesto 
eliminatetheconceptofaparticle…’.1Icannothelpfeelingthattheironyis 
misplaced:nobodywishesto‘eliminate’theconceptofaparticle,butsome 
peoplefelt,andstillfeel,thatitmayturnoutnottobeanultimateand 
irreduciblecategoryofphysics.Indeed,IshouldbesurprisedifPauliorBorn 
oranyothermemberoftheorthodoxyweretodenythis.Whythenattack 
Schrödingerforfirstopeningupthisproblem,andforfirstopeningoureyes 
tothepossibilityofunstableparticles?Explainingathingisnotexplainingit 
away. 


Tostatebrieflymyownopinionwithregardtothefirstquestion,Ifeel 
inclinedtosaythatSchrödinger’stheoryaccordingtowhichparticlesare 
fairlysimplewavestructures(‘wavepackets’)istoosimple,andisrefutedby 
thecharacterofparticletransition.Particlestructuresaremorecomplex,I 
think,andthewavepacketshouldbeinterpretedasdeterminingpropensities 
(asexplainedbelow)ratherthanasadescriptionoftheparticle’sstructure. 
Butthisoranyotherviewofthemattermust,ifatalltenable,beheavily 
indebtedtoSchrödinger’strulyrevolutionarytheoryofparticles.(Cp.section 
26,below.) 


Theanswertothesecondquestion—whethertheexistenceofparticlesis 
impliedintheinterpretationofthepresentquantumtheory—ofcourse 
dependsupontheinterpretationchosen.Born,PauliandHeisenbergclearly 
implythattheexistenceofparticlesispartoftheinterpretationwhichthey 
accept,whichistheorthodoxorCopenhageninterpretation,andthatthey 
differinthispointfromSchrödinger.Ifindthisalittlesurprising;forpriorto 
thiscontroversyoverSchrödinger’sarticle‘ArethereQuantumJumps?’I 
shouldhavethoughtthattheCopenhagenviewonthismatterwasvery 



different.Ishouldhavethoughtthat,accordingtothisview,particlesand 
waveswerenothingbuttwokindsof‘pictures’whichweuseinorderto 
applythequantum-theoreticalformalism,andwhichareneededsinceevery 
applicationmustofnecessityuseclassicalmodelsandideas.Theconceptsof 
‘particles’andof‘waves’areusedjustbecausetheyaresuchclassicalideas. 
Astotheirreality,orexistence,IthoughtthattheCopenhageninterpretation 
adoptedtheviewthatthisquestionshouldnotbeasked,oratleastthatthe 
answerwouldbethatnothinglikeparticlesorwavesexists,andthatparticles 
andwavesareonlyclassicalconceptswithwhichwedescribecertainaspects 
(complementaryaspects)ofwhatevermayexist.This,Ithought,wasthe 
orthodoxview.Butyounevercantell. 


(Thisorthodoxyisevenmoreelusivethanorthodoxiesusuallyare.There 
isnoBibleavailableasyetforexegesis;thusexegesismustnotbeattempted 
byanybodyoutsidetheesotericorthodoxyitself.Andtheorthodoxexegesisis 
sometimesindistinguishablefromasurreptitiouschangeoffront.) 


Asagainstthisview,IshouldhavethoughtthatSchrödingerassertedwith 
greatforcetherealexistenceofatomsorparticles,eventhoughhetriedto 
explainthemaswavestructures.Ididnotknowthat,bysayingthatatableor 
achairismadeoftimber,IshouldexposemyselftothecriticismthatIhave 
deniedtheexistenceofdiscretetablesandchairs,andthatIhavedoneso 
becauseIam‘inlove’withtimber(asBornsaysofSchrödinger’sattitude 
towardswaves2). 


NorcanIrecallthatinthelonghistoryofthecontroversyconcerningthe 
wavetheoryoflight,anybodyeversaidthatthosewhomaintainedthis 
theorywerecommittedtotheviewthatlight—ormorecorrectly,raysoflight 
—didnotexist.Thecaseisperfectlyanalogous,sinceSchrödingerexplained 
the 
transition 
from 
particle 
mechanics 
to 
wave 
mechanics 
as 
astep 
correspondingpreciselytothetransitionfromtheraytheoryoflight 
(geometricaloptics)tothewavetheory. 


ButIshallnotcontinuetoinvestigatetheattitudeofthevarious 
interpretersoftheexistingquantumtheorytowardsourproblem‘Arethere 
particles?’.InsteadIshallindicatebrieflywhattheanswerwouldbefromthe 
pointofviewofthepropensityinterpretation. 



Theanswerwouldbethis.Thepresentquantumtheoryis,viewedfrom 
thepropensityinterpretation,aparticletheory.Thewavesmerelydetermine 
theprobablestatesoftheparticle,thatistosay,theprobabilityorthe 
propensityoftheparticle’sbeinginacertainplace,orpossessingacertain 
momentum;exactlyasdothewavesinthesoldier’srandomwalk.Thewaves 
donotconstitutetheparticles,anymorethanthewavesintherandomwalk 
exampleconstitutethesoldier.Butjustasthewavesintherandomwalk 
examplearecharacteristicoftheconditionsofthesituation,orofthewhole 
experimentalset-up,soarethewavesofthequantumtheory. 


Itherefore 
declare 
myself 
unambiguously 
in 
favour 
of 
aparticle 
interpretation,althoughIaminlovewithwaves.Idonotfindany 
contradictionbetweenbeinginlovewithanidea—believingthatthereisvery 
muchtobelearnedfromit—andasserting,atthesametime,thatthisidea 
doesnotfitacertainproblemsituation.Schrödinger’swavetheoryofthe 
particleisfullofpromise,anditmarksthebeginningofatheoryofparticles. 
Yetatthesametime,thepresenttheorycanbemosteasilyinterpretedasa 
particletheory. 


Toavoidanymisunderstanding,IwishtomakeclearthatIdonotbelieve 
inadualismofparticlesandwavesoranythingevenfaintlyresemblingit.As 
tothepilotwavesofdeBroglie,theycan,Isuggest,bebestinterpretedas 
wavesofpropensities. 


15.PositionSpace. 


Theideaofa‘particle’haschanged,wehaveseen,fromthatofsomething 
ultimateandindestructibletothatofsomethingchangingandinneedof 
explanation.Whythendowecontinuetousetheterm‘particle’?The 
questionisnotimportant,butalsonotwhollywithoutinterest,inviewofthe 
protestswhichSchrödinger’sallegeddisbeliefinparticleshasaroused. 


Thestronginclinationtospeakof‘particles’(whichIshare)isdoubtless 
connectedwiththeintuitiveideathataparticleissomethinglocatedatany 
momentinasmallregionofspace,whoseconnectedtrackinspacemay,in 
principle,befollowedthroughitsvariouspositions.By‘space’Idonotmean 
here‘momentumspace’,or‘space-time’,oranyabstractspace,butordinary 



or,aswemaysay,positionspace. 


AparticletrackinaWilsoncloudchamber,orinaPowellstackof 
photographicplates(whichisfundamentallythesamething)isthekindof 
trackinpositionspacethatIhaveinmind;thusWilsonchambersandPowell 
stacksareessentiallypartsofpositionspace. 


Itisnotwithoutinteresttopointoutthatallourexperimentswith 
particles,andmoregenerallywithquantummechanicalsystems,consistin 
interpretingmeasurementsofpositions.Thisassertioniseventrivial,asa 
moment’sthoughtwillmakeclear.Allmeasurementsofvelocitiesor 
momentaconsistessentiallyininterpretingmeasurementsofposition.Thisis 
eventrueofrunners,motorcars,oraeroplaneswhosevelocitieswemaybe 
abletoestimatebysight;ifwewishtomeasuretheirvelocities,weshall 
eithermeasuretwopositions,orreadoffthepositionofapointerona 
speedometer.Withatomicorsubatomicparticles,thesituationispreciselythe 
same.Inmeasuringmomentum,wemeasureposition;andasarule,more 
thanonepositionisneeded. 


Thisistrueofanymethodusingspectroscopes,foritisthefinalposition 
ofthephotonwhichisindicativeofitsfrequencyormomentum;anditisso 
evenifweuseafilter:theessentialpointisthattheparticlehaspassedthe 
filter,whichisaquestionofitslocationinpositionspace. 


BohrhassaidsomethingveryclosetothepointIwishtomake.Forhe 
writes‘…itmustberealizedthat…[the]useofspace-timeconceptsinthe 
descriptionofatomicphenomenaisconfinedtotherecordingofobservations 
whichrefertomarksonaphotographicplate,ortosimilar…amplification 
effectslikethebuildingofwaterdropsaroundanioninacloudchamber’.1 
Bohr’sremarkcontainsatimelyreminderthateventhe‘observation’ofapart 
ofthetrackoftheparticleinpositionspaceis,likeall‘observations’,actually 
aninterpretationinthelightoftheories,inspiteofitsapparentintuitive 
obviousness.Themeasurementofthemomentumoftheparticlewilltherefore 
beaninterpretationofaninterpretation;thatis,aninterpretationofhigher 
order.(Itmaybesaidinpassingthatallourmeasurementsareinterpretations 
ofaveryhighorder;sohigh,indeed,thatweoftencannotevencomparethe 
interpretationsastowhethertheyareofhigherorlowerorder.) 



Thefactthatallmeasurementsofmomentumgobacktomeasurementsof 


positionis,asIshallexplaininthenextchapter,ofconsiderablesignificance 


foranevaluationofHeisenberg’sso-called‘PrincipleofIndeterminacy’.
